Lord Howard of Rising: My Lords, I thank the noble Lord, Lord Lea of Crondall, for securing this debate, and noble Lords on all sides of the House, for their interesting contributions. The noble Lord, Lord Lea, asked me to comment on the young Etonians. As an old Etonian, rather than a young one, I would not be impertinent enough to enter that frame.
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 102
 

Impressive as all the arguments have been, I think it is worthwhile examining the question itself. It is a matter of both economic and social concern that there are still people living in poverty in the United Kingdom, as there are in many other countries of the world, especially those to which the noble Lord, Lord Lea, referred. No one seeks massive inequalities in the distribution of wealth. It is tempting to suppose that the crux of the issue lies in the distribution of wealth, but surely there is a greater virtue in improving the prosperity and well-being of the less-well-off members of society, rather than worrying about relative wealth. It is much more important for the poor to become better off than to achieve equality by making the rich poorer. That process has been proved to reduce the prosperity of all, in spite of what the noble Lord, Lord Giddens, has said. Surely the criterion on which globalisation should be judged is whether it has raised the general standards of wealth and prosperity for the greatest number of people and whether it has contributed to an improved life for the poorer members of society.

Globalisation has come about from the removal of trade barriers and tariffs. Their progressive removal, often against much opposition, has encouraged international trade, which in recent years has been accelerated by the development of technology. Trade barriers were created to protect vested interests from outside competition which would force prices lower and create pressure to improve quality. With no outside competition, prices could be maintained at artificially high levels and there was no impetus to produce better quality goods. The effect of those higher prices was to restrict the availability of the protected goods and services to the better off. The poor could not afford, or would find it difficult, to pay higher prices. It is very much to its shame that the European Union persists in the practice of trade barriers, notably in agriculture. If it were not for the historic protection of European farmers against the forces of competition from elsewhere in the world, the price of food could tumble by as much as 50 per cent. It is an example of how restrictive trade practices work to keep prices up.

Where trade has not been obstructed, the less well off have benefited. With the reduction in tariffs and trade barriers came globalisation, but with globalisation there was a massive increase in the availability of cheaper and higher-quality goods, as well as the arrival in this country of international corporations, as pointed out by the noble Lord, Lord Bhattacharyya. To see how consumers benefit, your Lordships need only think of the impact on all our lives: mobile telephones, shoes, clothes, cars, cameras, televisions—an endless succession of goods. The same competition will help to reduce costs in service industries.

It is impossible to tell precisely and objectively whether the abolition of trade barriers and the resulting globalisation has, in the short term, created greater inequality of income and wealth in the United Kingdom. It has certainly made a massive
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 103
 
contribution to the improvement in the well-being of the less well off, who can afford things their parents and grandparents could only have dreamt of.

Globalisation, in spite of its faults, has been a force for good. Far from reversing the long-term trend towards equality, globalisation has been one of its driving forces and it would have been prevented or reduced by maintaining trade barriers. Where trade barriers have been maintained, it is the poor who pay the price, from farmers in Africa to Mr Mandelson's proposed tariff on leather shoes, which will restrict their availability to those on tight budgets.

The contributions to the debate have been impressive. I doubt whether there is much division over our objectives. We all want a more prosperous world. We all want an increasing number of people to enjoy the affluence that most take for granted.

8.50 pm

Lord McKenzie of Luton: My Lords, I start by thanking all noble Lords who have contributed to today's debate. It is a matter we could have discussed all week and still have had things left to say. I would particularly like to thank my noble friend Lord Lea for tabling the Question. The House will readily recognise that for him these matters are not just of passing academic interest, and that he has given distinguished service in the cause of greater equality over many years. It has been a wide-ranging debate; we have covered cricket, football and bank robberies, as well as more fundamental economic matters.

There is no standard definition of globalisation. Typically the term is used to describe the increasing integration of world markets. Technological progress and the rise of emerging markets have driven increasing international flows of goods and services, including finance, to which my noble friend Lord Desai made reference; people and resources; and ideas and culture. Most commentators judge that the present wave of globalisation started in the late 1980s. Globalisation and technological progress are the key drivers to rising prosperity in the UK, the EU and the rest of the world. Over the last 50 years world trade, which the noble Lord, Lord Howard, spoke about, has expanded some 75 times.

Globalisation has the potential to generate substantial economic benefits. Production and resources can be allocated around the world in the most efficient manner. Technology can also be distributed more widely, increasing the productivity of the world's workers. My noble friend Lord Bhattacharyya spoke with great conviction and expertise on this matter. Developing countries are also now playing an increasing role, and accounted for 29 per cent of world trade in 2001. Indeed, one of the key successes of the UK's recent presidency of the G7 was to convince the other economic powers to commit to further trade liberalisation so that developing countries can grow faster. Emerging economies account for an increasing share of global output, trade and investment.
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 104
 

The Government are committed to promoting further economic openness, and to an ambitious pro-development conclusion to the current Doha development agenda round of trade talks by the end of this year. If all merchandise and agricultural trade were freed, the world economy would be boosted by nearly $300 billion per year by 2015—more, if trade in services were also liberalised. Properly managed—as the right reverend Prelate reminded us, it is not just a question of letting the tide come in—globalisation will also help the Government to achieve their economic targets of improving the UK's productivity, and thus people's welfare and prosperity. It will encourage UK firms to concentrate on those sectors of the economy where we have an advantage over our international competitors. It will give firms access to new technology and reward them for their innovations.

Globalisation will also provide consumers with a greater choice of goods and services at lower prices. Around the world, those countries most open to the global economy are growing the fastest. We also need further globalisation to help developing countries and provide them with access to rich countries' markets. In agriculture alone, barriers prevent developing countries earning up to $75 billion a year, significantly more than current aid flows. That is why the G7 under the UK presidency agreed to work to further liberalise world trade.

Lower trade barriers to poor countries is a necessity, but not a sufficient condition for those countries to reach their potential. Problems of capacity also need to be addressed, such as trade-related infrastructures like roads, ports and customs, and, more importantly, the basic systems that help to keep populations healthy and well skilled. To help countries invest in capacity, the Government have increased aid flows, and UK aid will reach nearly £6.5 billion a year by 2007-08. In 2005, under the UK presidencies of the EU and the G8, the international community has committed to raise an additional $50 billion of aid by 2010, and to cancel 100 per cent of the multinational debts of the world's poorest countries.

Even though globalisation has the potential to benefit everyone, there have been increasing concerns, some expressed tonight, about its impact. People fear the changes globalisation will cause, even though these changes are rational and probably inevitable in the long run. As this debate shows, there are worries that while globalisation might generally promote faster economic growth, this may be at the expense of greater inequality. Inequality in the UK has risen since the 1970s. During the 1980s the income gap between high and low earners grew significantly. The gap stabilised in the 1990s and has since shown no firm upward or downward trend. Several noble Lords referred to that, including the noble Lord, Lord Newby, the noble Lord, Lord Lea of Crondall, himself, and the noble Lord, Lord Whitty. Some academics have recently found tentative evidence that the income of the poorest people has started to recover slightly, relative to top earners. However, the gap between top earners and those in the middle of the distribution has continued to widen. The relatively
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 105
 
better performance of the low paid could possibly be due to such measures as tax credit, referred to by a number of speakers today.

I would like to deal with the issue raised, in particular by the noble Lord, Lord Lea of Crondall, about the pay and pensions of top directors. My noble friend Lord Giddens also posed questions about this. It is crucial that our top businesses have top managers if they are going to perform internationally and if our economy is to grow. For companies to attract, retain and motivate people of the right calibre and experience, they have to pay the going international rate. However, it is important that directors are held to account for their actions and that their pay reflects their performance. World-class pay should demand world-class performance. Ultimately, it is for the companies' owners—the shareholders—to judge whether this is the case. It is not for the Government to determine or dictate directors' levels of pay.

However, the Government do have a role in ensuring that a framework is in place that allows shareholders to exercise their influence effectively on companies' remuneration policies. That is why the Government introduced the Directors' Remuneration Report Regulations 2002 to improve transparency and make directors more accountable to shareholders, and why we are working with the Financial Reporting Council in implementing the Combined Code on Corporate Governance. These are matters I know we will debate further during the passage of the Company Law Reform Bill. Recent data shows that this approach is having some impact in moderating pay at the top and certainly making it more closely related to performance.

Wealth inequality has also continued to widen and is greater than income inequality. We would expect the distribution of wealth to be more unequal than that of income, because a large part of wealth inequality is due to the accumulation of houses and other assets as people age. As such, much of the wealth gap is between young and old people, not rich and poor. Economists, however, are unsure of the role globalisation has played in the widening of inequality. My noble friend Lord Giddens challenged this mantra. Much of the rise in equality is due to workers with the skills and education wanted by today's economy receiving much higher wages. Although the income of workers without the new skills is still rising, some groups have fallen behind over the last 25 years. The academic consensus seems to be that the growth in this skills premium is mostly due to technological progress. Workers today need to know how to use the new technology, particularly the new information technology. There is little evidence linking globalisation, specifically, to rising inequality. It is also interesting to note that while many people seek to stop globalisation because of fears about inequality, few have sought to slow the development of new technologies, which seem to play more of a role in widening the income gap.

While this Government are committed to globalisation, we also recognise that it can create challenges for some groups. Even as the economy
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 106
 
expands and exploits the opportunities unlocked through greater trade, some businesses will find it difficult to deal with increased international competition. Businesses in the low-skill sectors will find the competition particularly intense. This inevitably means that some workers will leave present jobs and will need to find work elsewhere. They may find that they need new skills to compete. Without proper economic management globalisation could lead to greater inequality but that does not mean that we should attempt to turn back globalisation and protect our businesses from the rest of the world. Experience in this and other countries has shown that protectionist policies are costly and ultimately ineffective. Restricting access to our country's markets would also go against the commitments this country has made to the developing countries.

The right reverend Prelate the Bishop of Worcester said that humankind should take control of this process. I agree. The approach the Government have adopted is to increase flexibility in the economy so that business and workers can respond to new opportunities and shift the expanding areas of our economy. It is critical to ensure that flexibility is accompanied by fairness through polices which support individuals through periods of change. The New Deal in particular has helped over 3 million people find work. In parallel with changes to free up the economy, social policies have been introduced that provide support without blocking change. The tax credit is one example. As a result of personal tax and benefit measures introduced since 1997, from April 2006 families with children will on average be £1,500 a year better off and families with children in the poorest fifth of the population will be on average £3,350 a year better off. To spread wealth more widely the Government seek to make saving, assets and wealth ownership accessible to all and so give opportunity to the many, not the few.

My noble friend Lord Whitty posed a question about procedural matters. He asked whether the Government had to assess the impact on inequality of new initiatives that came forward. That is an interesting proposition. In some respects it would be very easy to measure the impact on inequality of a measure such as a tax change, a tax credit or a pension credit change. The impact of investment in education, science and skills is less easy to spot immediately. That is a dynamic process. However, it is an interesting thought that is worth developing.

In the longer term we need to ensure that this country has the skills needed to successfully compete on the international stage. To ensure our skills base the Government have invested in education and will continue to do so. Over the past 10 years the proportion of graduates in the labour force has grown from 19 per cent to 26 per cent. Since 1994 the number of people with no qualifications has fallen from 21 per cent to 14 per cent.

If measures are taken to increase the country's flexibility to adjust to change, including investment in infrastructure and better regulation, and by investing in our skills, innovation and science base,

globalisation can drive growth in this country, as it is around the world, without eroding the progress this Government have made on reducing poverty and tackling inequality.

Baroness Miller of Chilthorne Domer moved Amendment No. 307:

    Page 22, line 28, leave out subsection (2) and insert—

27 Feb 2006 : Column 108
 

In the same way as one would expect members of any other local authority to either live or work within the area they represent, we believe that this should equally apply to those representing a national park. At the moment, a national park member may live 40 or 50 miles or more from the park, even though they are an indirectly elected member. While many may absolutely have the parks' best interest at heart and may do their best at representing it, there is no doubt that it makes a difference if you do not live or work within the park. We expect people to be representative of their local authority because they understand how that community works—they understand its wishes and desires. How are national park members different from local authority members? It is true that you could end up with the parish council members from within the park, but those who are from the principal authorities—the district and county councils—could live outside the park. Then, there are the national members as well, so you could end up with a situation where very few people either lived or worked within the park but could still be on the national park authority. The two amendments are on an interesting subject. I garner three general principles raised by them, and I shall address them before I make some comments on the practicability of Amendment No. 307 itself. The first general principle, which is raised by both amendments, is that the primary legislation should specify that national members should always make up at least 25 per cent of an authority. The second general principle, again raised by both amendments, is that before laying an order to establish the membership of an authority the Government should consult not just the local authorities affected but,

    "all relevant authorities and interested bodies".

The third general principle, which is raised only by Amendment No. 307, tabled by the noble Baroness, Lady Miller, is that national park authorities should include some members—perhaps a majority—who are directly elected to that authority.

Let me take those three principles in turn. First, I refer to the 25 per cent rule. That is the case for specifying in primary legislation that 25 per cent of each national park authority must always be made up of those other members whom the Secretary of State appoints, and who are often referred to as the national members to distinguish them from their colleagues who are drawn from local authorities or parish councils. The key consideration is not so much the 25 per cent figure itself, which is in any case consistent with current practice, but whether it is sensible for any level to be set in primary legislation, and whether that is a decision best left for secondary
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 113
 
legislation, when the make-up of each individual national park authority—one is different from another—is being set by statutory instrument.

The Government's clear view is that such decisions are best left to secondary legislation where they can be taken on a case-by-case basis, and can reflect the context in which NPAs are working at the time. The Welsh Assembly Government are preparing to consult on membership arrangements and have expressed the view that they would not want the Bill to pre-empt the decisions that they will take in light of their consultation.

The second principle is whether the Government should have to consult more widely before introducing secondary legislation. Although the existing statutory requirement is to consult only the local authorities, it is already Defra's practice to include other consultees who may have an interest. In practice we are already doing what the amendment seeks. Perhaps it could be argued that no change to the legislation is therefore needed.

I turn to the principle of directly elected members, which is again part of Amendment No. 307 but not Amendment No.307A. This is a well-worn issue, having recently been debated and rejected in another place. Directly elected members for England and Welsh park authorities was a subject discussed when the existing legislation was being enacted, and was looked at again in both the English and Welsh reviews. The issue has never commanded general support. Indeed, as the noble Baroness was frank enough to say at the start of her speech, neither the Association of National Park Authorities nor the Council for National Parks supports the idea.

National park authorities are unique bodies. We need to recognise that and devise our approach accordingly. They have many of the characteristics of local authorities, including being subject to most of the generic local government legislation. They also have some features of a non-departmental public body. That duality reflects their dual responsibilities. They serve the country as a whole—they are after all national assets and called national parks—but they also have a direct responsibility towards those whose lives are spent in the park. The noble Lord talked about them in his speech.

The current arrangements reflect their unique role rather well. At present, each authority has some members who have been elected to local authorities in the area, some members who have been recruited through a national competition, and in England some members who have been elected to parish councils. That mix gives them a broad range of skills and expertise that they perhaps need. It also ensures that they do not overlook either their national or local responsibilities. Is it na-ve to say that the real test of the current system is whether or not it actually works? It seems to us that it does. It certainly seemed to work to the authors of the 2002 Defra review of English national park authorities, who said that they were not persuaded that directly elected members would bring clear benefits. The separate review of the Welsh
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 114
 
national park authorities also found no consensus on this issue. I have already mentioned what the Welsh Assembly Government plan in terms of consultation. In short, and to end, we see no need for a directly elected component. We believe that to introduce one could unbalance the approach that has proved successful for many years since independent national park authorities were established.

9.30 pm

Moving on from general principles, there are some drafting weaknesses in Amendment No. 307, which we fear is ambiguous and would not be workable. Lord Bach: Clause 58 serves to remove an uncertainty in the existing legislation. Section 62(1) of the Environment Act 1995 sets out the duty of national park authorities on socio-economic development. It makes it clear that their socio-economic work should be linked to their purposes and should be carried out in co-operation with other bodies. It also says that they should not incur "significant expenditure" on this activity, but the word "significant" is nowhere defined. Defra's 2002 review of NPAs recommended that the uncertainty should be removed by deleting the reference to "significant expenditure". We are now implementing that recommendation. We are doing the same for AONBs in paragraph 165 of Schedule 11.

The alternative approach would have been to set some specific limit on what national park authorities might spend, but we felt that that would not be reasonable given the different circumstances of national park authorities. By way of example, Northumberland has by far the smallest population and so a small budget. Precisely because it is so sparsely populated it may have a greater proportionate need to spend on socio-economic matters than larger national park authorities. Equally,
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 117
 
a limit might have been seen as a target—something that authorities should spend up to—which is not our intention either.

Our aim is rather to give the authorities the flexibility they need to support sustainable development in their areas; we believe that removing the financial constraint does that. I want to emphasise that other constraints remain; namely, that national park authorities' socio-economic work should be linked to their purposes and should be carried out in co-operation with other bodies. That is in our view a neutral measure that simply removes an ambiguity and leaves responsibility for setting socio-economic spending levels with the national park authorities where it best belongs. It certainly does not imply any increase in grant at all and I agree exactly with what my noble friend Lord Judd said.

    "an emergency or disaster occurs involving destruction of or danger to life or property and, as a result, one or more local authorities incur expenditure on, or in connection with, the taking of immediate action . . . to safeguard life or property, or to prevent suffering or severe inconvenience, in their area or among its inhabitants".

Section 155(4) lists the categories of local authority that may be covered by such a scheme. County councils, which managed the national parks at the time of the 1989 legislation, are on the list of eligible authorities. However, in 1997—1996 in Wales—responsibility for the parks passed to independent national park authorities, but these new bodies were
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 120
 
not added to the list of eligible bodies. That treatment reflected a view that only charging or major precepting authorities—in other words, those authorities with a separate formula spending share under the RSG system—should be eligible. It also reflects the reality that national park authorities will rarely be responsible for the emergency phase of any incident.

Baroness Farrington of Ribbleton: This clause introduces a provision for biosphere reserves selected by the UK and designated under UNESCO's Man and the Biosphere programme, to be given pilot status with regard to planning and ministerial guidance. I am grateful to the noble Baroness for raising the profile and awareness of the subject of biosphere reserves, UNESCO sites which aim to reconcile the conservation of biodiversity with its sustainable use.

At present the three English sites—Braunton Burrows; Moor House, Upper Teesdale and the North Norfolk Coast, and the other five UK sites for that matter—do not fully meet the UNESCO criteria in that they are primarily designated for just a core zone and none has included complete buffer zones or transition zones indicated as necessary under the 1995 revised UNESCO criteria. This reflects the UK's approach to the selection and protection of wildlife sites whether they be at a local level, for instance national nature reserves, at national level, SSSIs or internationally—Natura 2000 and Ramsar sites. For each of these, our approach has been to identify the main populations that require protection and draw a tight line around those to ensure that all parties know the area merits protection.

Whereas many other EU member states and countries around the world have included within their statutory designations buffer zones and/or transition zones, as highlighted by the UNESCO designations, the UK's regulatory regime is such that this is not a necessary requirement to achieve the sustainable conservation protection of the sites. The UK's application of the EC Habitats Directive's network of sites, Natura 2000, highlights this approach.

While Natura 2000 sites encompass the important habitats or species of concern, controls on activities that may impact on these are not limited by where they occur but by where the affect may be felt. In other words, the UK's approach is that the potential buffer zone to its protected area network is the wider countryside in general.
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column 123
 

Although, as speakers have recognised, a further review of the UK's network of biosphere reserves is due, I do not at this stage see any wildlife benefit from extending zonations. As the core area of the UK's current crop of biosphere reserves is already statutorily protected, additional designation by UNESCO appears unlikely to result in additional direct wildlife gain. That said, I recognise that indirect benefit may derive from this international accolade, as it may encourage other stakeholders to give greater consideration to their actions and their consequences. The regional approach of biosphere reserves also reflects landscape-scale approaches to conservation found in conservation policies at global to local levels, and can contribute to the delivery of biodiversity action plan objectives.

On the basis that the current statutory designations on which the UK's biosphere reserves have been selected are already included within existing planning and ancillary ministerial guidance—this is unlikely to change—I do not believe that the proposed amendment will achieve any wildlife gain. On the contrary, it may actually increase duplication and red-tape which could detract from the consideration of the important wildlife sites on which the reserves are based. Nevertheless, the noble Baroness and my noble friend Lord Judd have raised some important points which I will ensure that Natural England will explore.

On the basis that the aims of the biosphere reserves are already adequately addressed by the protection afforded to the statutory designations at their core, I hope the noble Baroness will feel able to withdraw her amendment. If, on reflection, having read the debate, I have anything to add, I shall, of course, write to noble Lords.

Company Law Reform Bill [HL]

(Fifth Day)

Links to other Grand Committee Sittings on the Bill

The Deputy Chairman of Committees (Lord Elton): If there is a Division in the Chamber while we are sitting, this Committee will adjourn as soon as the Division Bell rings and resume after 10 minutes.

Clause 239 [Derivative claims]:

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts moved Amendment No. 246A:

    Page 108, line 13, leave out paragraph (b).

The noble Lord said: With this amendment we come to Part 11 of the Bill, which is entitled "Derivative Claims and Actions". This is another area that has caused some concern and about which we have had a good many representations from business organisations, such as the CBI, and from practitioners, such as leading firms of City solicitors. In particular, I put on record my thanks for the very helpful expert briefing that we have had from Maitland Chambers, in the shape of Mr Matthew Collings.

I find this quite a technical area, legally, as I am not a lawyer. I am not even an accountant—industry and commerce are where I have lived my life. Therefore, it would be very unwise of me to trade legal punches with as persuasive and experienced an advocate as the noble and learned Lord the Attorney-General. In my experience, when one takes a Bill through this House, one usually finds some legal precedent, which is repeated like a mantra by the cognoscenti. When I was doing the Charities Bill, it was Re Resch, which was a dense piece of legislation. It was never quite clear to me why an Australian case involving private hospitals was the seminal case for public benefit tests in the United Kingdom, but let that be as it may.

With regard to these provisions, the important case seems to be Foss v Harbottle. I am sure that the noble and learned Lord has probably forgotten more about that case than I shall ever know, so I shall cut to the chase in summarising what we see as the basic disagreements over Part 11.

The Government have argued that the part as drafted does not change the law at all. That was the view expressed by the Minister, Mr Alun Michael, in his letter to the Financial Times, on Wednesday 9 November 2005, in which he said:

    "It should not give rise to concern on the part of the millions of directors in this country who have high standards of conduct and make such an important contribution to Britain's economy".

That line was followed through by the noble and learned Lord in Committee on 6 February, when he said:
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC2
 

    "We will come to Part 11, which deals with derivative claims and action by members, describing the mechanisms under which shareholders can enforce the duties on behalf of the company. I can say now that we do not see any reason why those provisions should increase litigation".—[Official Report, 6/2/06; cols. GC242-3.]

The representations made to us suggest a contrary view, and our amendments, all probing at this stage, are designed to test the Government's position and legal reasoning. I hope that in that way all interested parties will be able to see the two sides of the argument. In the absence of some Damascene conversion—and I am having some trouble finding the road to Damascus on this issue—I hope that after Committee stage has finished the noble and learned Lord the Attorney-General might be prepared to have a meeting outside the Committee at which some of the more technical legal issues could be thrashed out to see if some common position with which everybody is comfortable can be reached. I rather doubt that it is helpful to swap heavyweight detailed technical arguments about derivative actions in Committee.

I am sorry if I have irritated the noble Lord, Lord Sainsbury, by tabling these amendments—and I understand his irritation. After all, he no doubt thinks that after eight years people might have made all their points before getting to Committee; but that is not how it has turned out. To put it in vulgar, non-legal terms, people who have spoken to us fear a double whammy. In Part 10, directors' duties are widened, while Part 11 makes it easier for shareholders to commence actions against directors. There is concern. Is it justified? That is what we are trying to find out in Committee.

I turn to the specifics of Amendment No. 246A, which concerns the first clause in Part 11—Clause 239, on derivative claims. As we understand it, Part 11 is intended to put in statute the exceptions to the rule in Foss v Harbottle. Indeed, paragraph 480 of the Explanatory Notes refers to,

    "a new procedure for bringing such an action which set down criteria for the court distilled from the Foss v Harbottle jurisprudence".

However, a number of representations made to us claim that this risks enlarging the scope of such derivative claims. Many have argued that Clause 239, rather than mirroring the common law as the Government claim, in fact goes further. The common law is uncertain. These provisions have not been much used and the outcomes have sometimes been conflicting, so it is hard to specify exactly what the current position is.

These clauses seem to be mostly modelled on the "fraud on the minority" exception, where shareholders are permitted to bring a claim against the wrongdoers in the name of the company where the wrongdoers are in control of the company, so that the proper claimant, being the company, is improperly prevented from bringing a claim against them. There are other exceptions, such as where the act complained of is ultra vires or where it infringes the personal right of a shareholder. However, these seem more remote from the procedure proposed in the Bill.
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC3
 

It appears from the case law—I am quoting from Megarry Vice-Chancellor in Estmanco (Kilner House) Ltd v GLC from Tolley's Company Law, paragraph S4032—that fraud for these purposes comprises,

    "fraud for the wider equitable sense of that term, as in equitable concept of fraud on a power".

Tolley also suggests in the same paragraph that fraud in this sense does not include pure negligence. Therefore, it seems that Clause 239, in allowing claims involving negligence, may in fact go further than the common law.

Overall, given the uncertain nature of the common law position, and since the provisions of Clause 239 cannot possibly take account of all the nuances reflected in the line of cases on exceptions to the rule in Foss v Harbottle, it is fair to say that the new statutory procedure may make it easier for shareholders to bring claims.

I have not, of course, had the benefit of seeing the Attorney-General's speaking note, but I dare say that one of the arguments that he will develop is that the Government introduced this codification in the light of the recommendation to do so by the Law Commission. However, it is important to remember that this recommendation pre-dated the Company Law Review final report as well as the proposed new clauses relating to directors of companies in Part 10, which we have discussed previously. In particular, it pre-dated the concept of enlightened shareholder value that the Government have proposed to enshrine in the Bill.

In practice, derivative claims are exceedingly rare. It has been put to us that this codification, if passed as currently worded, will have a minimal positive benefit of clarifying a rarely used piece of law while having the damaging and far more significant effect of increasing shareholder litigation and reducing the number of people willing to take directorships.

This first amendment would remove subsection (2)(b). Section 459 of the Companies Act 1985 provides a separate cause of action to shareholders where their company's affairs have been conducted in an "unfairly prejudicial" manner. Section 461 then gives the court wide powers to grant relief in respect of the matter complained of. There is, therefore, no place or need for a derivative claim as provided in Clause 239(2)(b) of the Bill—the court has all the appropriate jurisdiction under Section 461.

It has been argued to us that the existence of Section 459 is another reason why the codification of derivative claims need not take place: there is already a working method whereby aggrieved shareholders can exercise their rights. If this codification goes ahead, the Bill should not muddy the waters with such a reference to Section 459, which is a stand-alone procedure unrelated to derivative claims.

Amendment No. 249A seeks to make it absolutely clear that the jurisdiction of Section 459 and the process it provides do not in any way affect the new statutory basis for derivative claims. As I have said, there appears to be no need for a statutory basis for
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC4
 
derivative claims, as they can effectively be brought under Section 459. It is obviously unfairly prejudicial for a director to act in breach of duty and cause loss to a company, and he can be made liable to compensate the company pursuant to an order under Section 461. The case law of Lowe v Fahey in 1996 is relevant here. We argue in these two amendments that it should be made absolutely clear that Section 459 will continue to operate as it currently does.

I am afraid that I have spoken at some length, but I felt that it would be helpful on the first amendment in this part to explain our fundamental misgivings. This will enable me to be a great deal briefer on later amendments. I beg to move.

The Attorney-General (Lord Goldsmith): I thank the noble Lord for explaining the nature of the concerns that he wants the Committee to explore. They do not come as a surprise to me, but it is helpful that the noble Lord has set them out in the way that he has. I shall adopt very much the same approach. I will address the detailed points as they arise under the amendments, but it may be helpful if I first make some more general comments about the Government's approach to this part of the Bill and seek to address the concerns that have been expressed about its operation. I say at once that the Government will be very happy to take up the request of the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, for a meeting outside the Committee in order to explore some of these matters further. I am happy to say that that invitation will be taken up.

The noble Lord is absolutely right to say that it is the Government's intention to implement the recommendation of the Law Commission, which goes back as far as 1997. I have got it here. The key recommendation was that there should be a new derivative procedure, with more modern, flexible and accessible criteria for determining whether a shareholder can pursue an action. Although it goes back a number of years, the clear majority of those who responded to the several consultations on the proposals for a statutory derivative procedure have accepted that the Law Commission's proposals have important advantages over the common law derivative action. The proposals make it clear that the procedure is potentially available as a means of enforcing the whole range of directors' duties—including statutory fiduciary duties and the duty of skill and care—and the procedural requirements for bringing a claim will be more accessible and more clearly set out.

So what of the concerns? First, the Bill does not introduce any major change of principle to the law in this area. Although, as the noble Lord said, the derivative claim is not at the moment frequently invoked, it is a well-established mechanism by which shareholders can, in certain circumstances, bring an action in the name of the company. Secondly, it is a fail-safe mechanism rather than a weapon of first resort. It is important to remember—I have made the point previously—that the damages are paid not to individual shareholders but to the company itself, and yet it is the shareholders, the members who bring the action, who may be required to bear heavy legal costs.
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC5
 

Thirdly and importantly, there will continue to be tight judicial control of cases brought under the new procedure. Fourthly, a derivative action is not and will not be the same thing as an American-style shareholder class action brought in the name of a group of shareholders. Under the reforms of directors' liabilities, introduced by the Companies (Audit, Investigations and Community Enterprise) Act 2004, to which I referred during the previous Committee sitting, companies may already indemnify directors against any liability incurred in respect of such actions, even if judgment is given against the director.

3.45 pm

We will need to return later to the changes caused by the procedural reforms introduced by the noble and learned Lord, Lord Woolf, that adjust the way that derivative actions are brought at the moment and which affect some of the procedural mechanisms in the Bill.

What do we expect? I repeat that we do not expect there to be a significant increase in the number of derivative claims as a result of putting derivative action on a statutory footing—and the Law Commission did not expect that, either. There will continue to be tight judicial control of such cases and we would expect the judiciary to be circumspect when reaching decisions about applications; in particular, we would expect the judiciary to continue to take the view that a disagreement between members should usually be resolved under the company's constitution without recourse to the courts. The procedure that we have set out provides proper safeguards in that respect.

We also expect the courts to respect commercial judgments; the procedure that we impose will ensure that. It goes without saying—and this is what the Law Commission wanted—that there should be greater clarity about how a shareholder may bring a derivative action. The existing tests have been widely criticised and we firmly believe that they need to be replaced.

May there be cases where courts give permission for cases to continue which might not have been able to previously? Yes, there may be, but in cases where proper claims were improperly being frustrated, that would in general be a welcome development. Certainly I would not expect this to result in opening the floodgates or any other of the horrendous spectacles that I have seen mentioned in some places.

We have to strike a careful balance between protecting directors from vexatious and frivolous claims and protecting the rights of shareholders. It would be dangerous to move too far against either of those interests. Have the Government got the balance right? We believe that we have, but we shall explore that matter further in Committee and at subsequent stages.

Against that background, let me turn to the two amendments. Both relate to the procedures already available under Section 459 of the Companies Act 1985. I understood the noble Lord's point and I recognise that these are entirely probing amendments about the need to ensure that Section 459 will continue
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC6
 
to allow an application to be made for a derivative claim. That is what Clause 239(2)(b) achieves. It is common for a derivative claim to be brought under Section 459 in pursuance of an order of the court, but it is quite a lengthy and expensive procedure—the Law Commission had much to say about that.

We do not believe that the provision under Section 459 to permit a derivative action should be removed. We continue to believe that there should be two alternative routes by which a claim may be brought, either under Section 459 or under the new procedure in this part of the Bill. Under Section 459, the shareholder would be required to prove unfair prejudice. Neither amendment proposed by the noble Lord would improve the position. It is clear in the drafting that the availability of that route would remain.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am grateful to the noble and learned Lord for that full reply. I am sure that we will wish to avail ourselves of his kind invitation. I shall make just two points, the first of which I made in my opening remarks. The Law Commission's recommendation pre-dates quite a lot of events. That is one of the issues that have been brought to our attention. The world has moved on. How much the world has moved on in this technical area is hard for me to discern, but we would like to explore that outside Committee. My second point is the purpose behind the amendment. Therefore, to bring it under subsection (2) runs the danger of confusing two parallel possibilities for redress. Lord Goldsmith: I am grateful to the noble Lord for allowing me to take up that invitation. When one looks at the requirements in relation to derivative actions in the remainder of the part, it is clear that those are derivative claims under this chapter. The Section 459 procedure is in that respect left untouched. That is why I do not think that the clause muddies the waters in relation to Section 459. I say that in case it helps those who are assisting the noble Lord.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am grateful for that further clarification. We will come back to this matter at a later date outside Committee. In the mean time, I beg leave to withdraw the amendment.

The noble Lord said: The background to the amendment is a briefing that we have received from Allen & Overy, the well-known firm of City solicitors. The amendment would closely mirror the common law in that the proposed statutory action would be available only where the company has refused to seek appropriate redress. It is not in the interests of companies for members to be able to bypass the board's usual decision-making process in this way, which is likely to increase the risk of unmeritorious claims being brought for tactical reasons.

I anticipate that the Attorney-General will argue that this matter is covered by Clause 242(3)(e), but the argument has been put to us that that provision would come into force later in the process and that it would be better for this limitation to appear up front in the legislation. Lord Clinton-Davis: Before we hear the Attorney-General's reply, will the noble Lord indicate whether the request to directors should have been made orally or in writing? I would prefer it that the directors had been requested in writing by a member of the company.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am always delighted to take suggestions for amendments to my drafting from any corner of the Committee. If the noble Lord, Lord Clinton-Davis, believes that that would clarify the draft and make it better, I am happy to consider it.

Lord Goldsmith: That exchange illustrates one of the concerns I have about the amendment as it stands, which is about placing rigid but possibly difficult-to-apply conditions on whether a derivative claim can be brought. One of the difficulties with the present law is the need for a shareholder to demonstrate, for example, that wrongdoers are in control—that is one of the ways in which a derivative claim may be brought. The shareholder has to allege and then demonstrate that those whose actions he complains of are in control of the company and will not permit its name to be used as claimant in the action. That can become a protracted issue because, as the Law Commission observed, it is not clear what "wrongdoer control" means. As long ago as 1962, the Jenkins Committee stated that it would be extremely difficult to devise a satisfactory general provision expressing this concept and the remainder of the exception to the rule in Foss v Harbottle in wider terms. That is one of the reasons why the Law Commission proposed a new statutory remedy.

Having to demonstrate wrongdoer control does not appear in the Government's approach, nor have we included in Clause 242 a requirement that the directors have been requested to bring a claim and have decided not to do so. That requirement might give rise to a dispute about whether a request had been made, perhaps going beyond the question of whether it was in writing or made orally to how clear it was and whether the shareholder put sufficient information
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC8
 
before the directors so that they were able to determine whether the claim should or should not have been brought. Furthermore, what is meant by,

    "have decided not to bring the claim"?

What would happen if the directors say, "We're not saying we won't, but we are not, at the moment, saying we will; we want to do this and that and hear the other"? One can see circumstances—a cynic might say that we would see them regularly—in which the directors might, without deciding not to bring the claim, spin it out in order to buy time.

We think it is better to set out the requirements which the court must have regard to under Clause 242(3). They include a number of matters, one being that the company has decided not to pursue the claim. In circumstances where the company has decided not to pursue the claim, the court will want to look at why it decided that. There are a number of possibilities. One would be that it had made a bona fide decision that the claim ought not to be pursued. Such a decision would be very influential with the court, which may decide not to allow the claim to go ahead. Alternatively, the company may have decided not to pursue the claim for reasons that the court finds unsatisfactory, which might have the opposite effect. It would be for the court to determine.

I would not want to bring in a rigid requirement of the sort that the noble Lord's amendment provides. However, civil procedure rules and pre-action protocols, which are now part of the new civil procedure world, might well require that some form of notice should be sent. That would be for after the Bill, but if notice were to be required, the court would, when deciding whether to grant permission, have regard to whether the pre-action protocol had been met and what its consequences had been. However, the Government are not minded to accept it as a requirement on the face of the Bill.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: Again, I am grateful to the noble and learned Lord the Attorney-General for that response.     "The cause of action may be against the director or another person (or both)".

It is not really clear to us how wide "another person" could be drawn. It seems to us that that drafting could leave people with a peripheral connection with the company in danger of having a derivative claim brought against them.

Lord Goldsmith: The amendment would have two effects. First, it would change the formulation in subsection (3), which is,

    "an actual or proposed act or omission involving negligence, default, breach of duty or breach of trust by a director of the company".

The formulation follows that suggested by the Law Commission and it seems to us that it better captures all the circumstances in which a director should be
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC10
 
liable for claims arising from breach of duty. The noble Baroness will, I hope, forgive me, as I do not at the moment fully understand what change she seeks to obtain with that part of the amendment. What the rest of the amendment does is much clearer; it would drop the sentence,

    "The cause of action may be against the director or another person (or both)".

That is the point to which the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, particularly spoke. That follows very clearly from the Law Commission's recommendation that, in this circumstance, a claim against a third party should be possible. However, the claim under this head should be allowed only if there has been a breach of duty by the director.

The Law Commission posed a couple of examples. One concerns circumstances where, by reason of a breach of duty by the director, a third party has come into possession of property of the company when, according to standard principles of law, it should be required to hand that property back—for example, the property has been transferred in breach of trust or the individual has been giving knowing assistance. In those circumstances, it seems to us right that it should be possible for a derivative claim to proceed not only against the director, but against the third party—otherwise the substantive and important belief would not be adhered to of getting back for the company from the third party the property that has been transferred. That is an example where it ought to be possible, in the Government's view and we think in the Law Commission's view, to have such a claim.

Another example given is that of a profitable company that is the victim of a tort by a third party. The directors might decide that they do not want to bring that claim against the third party. I ask the Committee to hypothesise that these directors, although otherwise committed to the well-being of the company, on this occasion do not wish—for bad reason and ulterior motive—to enforce the remedy for tort. They would in those circumstances be in breach of duty, but that breach of duty would not have given rise to the claim—in the words of the Bill, the claim is not

    "arising from an actual or proposed act or omission . . . by a director".

So it would not be possible in those circumstances for a member to bring a derivative claim against the third party.

I identify those two examples because they show why it is desirable that a claim should be capable of being brought in certain circumstances—although certainly not in every case—where the company has a claim against a third party and there has been at some point a breach of duty by the directors. Those are the reasons for wanting to keep the provision in the Bill. I should add that it needs to be clear whether it would be standard for the company to want to proceed—and therefore, in the derivative claim, for the shareholders to want to proceed—against both the director and the third party. It is important that the Bill should make it clear whether that could or could not happen.

Lord Goldsmith: I am grateful to the noble Lord for drawing my attention to part of what my noble friend had said which I had not fully addressed. This was a recommendation of the Law Commission which was quite clear; it was one of the areas upon which it consulted, and it reached a clear conclusion. There is a deficiency in the current law in relation to Foss v Harbottle; in certain circumstances, it would be possible to use the derivative claim where the complaint was of negligence by the directors but only, as I understand the law, if it could be shown that the directors—or at least the majority shareholders—had profited from the negligence, which would not always be the case. So there is a deficiency—there would certainly be cases of negligence by directors where a derivative claim could not be brought. It does not apply to any claim for negligence—in claims for negligence where the directors or the majority obtained a profit, it could be brought, but not otherwise. That is a change, but in the view of the Government and the Law Commission, it is a good change.

Lord Grabiner: The amendment, along with others that we shall examine later, is directed towards a legitimate concern—namely, the risk of proliferation of vexatious or near-vexatious litigation. It is said, rightly, that that would damage commercial activity. It is easy to think of some examples, which my noble friend Lady Goudie and other noble Lords have identified, such as the venture fund, working in cahoots with the rough equivalent of the ambulance-chasing solicitor, buying a few shares in a targeted company and then bringing a derivative claim, alleging that its rights as a minority shareholder have been abused by the controllers of the company.

Perhaps I may put another factor into the pot. There might be an additional incentive for such persons to make such claims because it is possible for the court to make an order that indemnity costs can be paid, so that the claimant can claim his costs out of the company and be indemnified fully in respect of those costs. That might be an added inducement to the kind of complainant that we are discussing.

These considerations focus attention on a key procedural question. What is the machinery for weeding out abusive or near-abusive claims? My immediate reaction to the suggestion in the amendment that only past or previous shareholders should be allowed the complaint was that that does not sound right, because you can buy and sell shares in the company on a daily basis. Once you buy shares, you are party to a changing contract and you derive all the benefits and rights associated with that contract. The fact that you arrive later rather than earlier on the scene should not in principle deprive you of the entitlement of that contractual bargain. That is not consistent with company law experience. I am more interested in focusing on how we weed out the abusive or near-abusive claim.

Perhaps I may say something about the existing procedure, because improvements to that have been suggested elsewhere in the Bill, and we will examine them in due course. First, in relation to indemnity costs, the law is clear that the court will not make an order for indemnity costs until a late stage in the proceedings when it has a good understanding of what the facts are. That is extremely good protection against the risk that a vulture fund might come along, get its costs protected from the outset and then bring an abusive claim. That was established as long ago as 1986 in Smith v Croft (No. 1).
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC14
 

I shall say something about civil procedure rule 19.9. I am conscious of the point made by the noble Lord, Lord Hodges, that this is technical legal territory. My point is technical and legalistic, but brief and clean. Under rule 19.9, the claimant, having commenced proceedings, is obliged to secure permission from the court to continue those proceedings. The application must be made before any other step is taken in the action. Having commenced proceedings, the claimant must make an application as the first thing he does. In the leading case, Barrett v Duckett 1995, the Court of Appeal laid down some general principles to be applied by the judge who decides whether to allow a case to continue. First, the alleged wrongdoer must have control of the company—in principle, that includes cases of de facto control because one could have de facto control with less than 50.1 per cent of the company. Secondly, the complaining shareholder must satisfy the court that he has a prima facie case and should be allowed to sue on behalf of the company. The mere fact that he can demonstrate that he has an arguable case will not be good enough. Thirdly, the complaining shareholder must also show that he is bringing the claim in good faith for wrongs done to the company in respect of which no other remedy exists. It follows that if the claim is brought for an ulterior purpose or if another adequate remedy is available, the claim would not be allowed to proceed.

The potential problem, which ought to be acknowledged, is that because the application to continue the proceedings has to be made immediately under the existing rules, there is a risk that the court may feel the need to make a ruling at a very early stage in the proceedings, on very flimsy material, before documentary disclosure or any exchange of witness statements. If that were the case, the danger is that a judge would be understandably reluctant to stop somebody at that early stage in the proceedings and might well give leave to continue the proceedings, whereas if he had been invited to address the question nine months or a year later when more material was available he might have thrown the case out on the basis that no prima facie case was disclosed in the material. This problem was identified by Mr Justice Collins in the 2003 case Konamaneni v Rolls-Royce (India). The key point is that there is nothing in CPR 19.9 that would prevent the judge from dealing with the point at a later stage, which would be consistent with Barrett v Duckett and Smith v Croft. There may well be scope for clarifying the rule and/or the notes to the practice book, in consultation with the rules committee. It would then be possible to produce a simple and satisfactory solution.

It would be very unattractive to cut across the well-established principle of company law that when one becomes a member of a company, one is party to the bargain. To superimpose a prohibition against going backwards in time upon that well-established rule is an unattractive solution, so I am not able to support this amendment.

Lord Goldsmith: I am grateful to my noble friend Lady Goudie for raising this matter and to everyone else who has spoken in the short debate on this

amendment—the noble Lords, Lord Hodgson and Lord MacGregor, and my noble friend Lord Grabiner. I am grateful because the amendment has provided a vehicle for a rather wider discussion on what is obviously a very important issue under this part of the Bill—that is, whether the procedural safeguards that we have built into the Bill will be sufficient, as we believe they will be, to protect against the concerns raised, which I accept are legitimate.

In his very helpful intervention, my noble friend Lord Grabiner indicated how the courts already use their powers to control vexatious, frivolous or otherwise inappropriate actions. Those are important points to consider. It is also important—here I respond directly to the noble Lord, Lord MacGregor, by repeating what I said previously—to ask what other things will prevent American-style class actions. This will be a claim, albeit brought by a shareholder—perhaps at his own expense—for damages to go to the company rather than to himself. There is an important difference in incentive there.

Fundamentally, this is a question of judicial control. On that broad topic, I apprehend that the meeting which the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, asked for at the beginning of today's sitting will be one where those issues can be explored a little further. Such a circumstance might be if the court adjourned the application while a general meeting took place. That might be a way of dealing with the point raised by the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, under another amendment about testing whether the company is prepared to take the action itself or, in some circumstances, to take some other action such as ratifying a particular act which has taken place.     "It is immaterial whether the cause of action arose before or after the person seeking to bring or continue the derivative claim became a member of the company".


 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC16
 

Of course, the claim has to be there. You cannot create it but, if it is there, it should not matter whether you became a member before or after it came into existence. I agree with my noble friend that company law works on the basis that, when you acquire shares, you get all the bad and all the good that go with them and sometimes perhaps a bit of both.

My second point is that the measure has the additional benefit of making it clear that it is the people who are members at the time that they wish to bring proceedings that bring those proceedings. You cannot sell on your shares and then seek to bring a derivative claim in the name of the company at that stage. So in principle it is right to have the position as stated in the Bill. I think that I am right in saying that some respondents, including the Law Society, wanted us to make it clear in the Bill that that was the position, following the current law. I cannot accept the amendment as it stands, but that does not detract from recognising that we have had an important debate on an important topic.

endeavour to use this procedure as a way of putting pressure on boards of directors. If we can cut those vexatious claims out at an early stage, that will be beneficial.

Lord Goldsmith: The Bill is drafted as it is because of changes to civil procedure as a result of the work of the noble and learned Lord, Lord Woolf. At present, a derivative claim is commenced by the issue of a claim form; it must generally be served within four months after it has been issued. There is no requirement to seek the permission of the court, but the rules provide that, after the claim form has been issued, the claimant must apply to the court for permission to continue the claim and the application must be supported by written evidence. No further steps may be taken until permission has been obtained. One does not need leave to start but one needs the court's leave to continue. The defendant is notified at the earliest possible time after the commencement of proceedings of the existence of those proceedings. The difference between having leave to start before and starting and having permission afterwards is not a major issue. One can just as easily apply a strong filter by the courts by requiring somebody to have permission to continue as by requiring them to obtain leave in advance.

The other question, referred to by the noble Lord, Lord Sharman, is whether it is a good idea to have a substantive hearing in relation to that with both sides present and a lot of information known—as my noble friend Lord Grabiner suggested in some cases—or whether it is better to do it at an earlier stage. There will be arguments both ways. The court would have the opportunity to determine that it is a case where the permission hearing should be pushed off awhile, in the circumstances I have described, or to deal with it then and there. The Law Commission's belief is that it is best to have a preliminary hearing of the issue of leave but that the court should receive all the pleadings before taking that decision. We would leave it to the courts to determine precisely what, in particular cases, they consider the right moment might be under the powers proposed in the Bill.

Under the procedures, it would also be possible in an appropriate case for a defendant to apply to strike out a claim. That power exists under the civil procedure rules, which will no doubt need to be amended to take account of the new derivative claim procedure. I see no reason why the general power to be able to strike out a claim should not be applicable as well.

The fundamental answer to the noble Lord is that the way in which the Bill is drafted so far as it concerns England and Wales reflects the new sort of procedure that will come in. I almost called the noble Lord, Lord Hodgson, "my noble friend", and I am happy to do so. In any event, the answer is as I have given.

Lord Goldsmith: To some extent we have covered this territory. The removal of the requirement for notice raises similar issues to those raised under Amendment No. 250. The Law Commission grappled with this issue because, under the common law, a shareholder seeking to bring a derivative action has to prove fraud and wrongdoer control and that the company is entitled to the relief claimed on a prima facie basis before being able to proceed.
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC22
 

The Law Commission canvassed views on whether there should be a threshold test on the merits of the case, which is what the amendment proposes, under the new procedure. The commission's concern was that, if one included an express test, that would increase the risk of a detailed investigation into the merits of the case taking place at the stage of leave or permission, and that such a mini-trial could be time-consuming and expensive. The commission accepted that there would have to be some consideration of merits, because it would be wrong for the court to allow a clearly hopeless case to proceed. As I have already mentioned, under the civil procedure rules the court has the power to dismiss a claim at an early stage that claim has no realistic prospect of success. But the Law Commission's view was that it would be undesirable to encourage the parties to bring evidence to show that the case met, or failed to meet, a particular merits test. There is a real risk of satellite litigation, which can balloon out of all proportion to the significance of what one is doing, if one adopts that approach. Most respondents to the consultation agreed with that view.

The Government's view is that there is a real risk, as the Law Commission said, that a threshold test would lead to fine distinctions being drawn about whether the facts of an individual case fell on one side or another of an individual test. Therefore, we prefer to leave, as Clause 242(3) does, a much broader set of requirements, which the court must take into account in deciding in its discretion whether to allow the case to proceed. Whether the court, when it develops its principled approach, decides to include some statement about a merits test generally to be reached or always to be reached would be for the court to develop. But, following the Law Commission's line, we are not disposed to build another threshold test into the statute.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am extremely grateful to the noble and learned Lord for that clear explanation. I appreciate the balance to be struck and certainly share his concern about "satellite litigation"—that is not a phrase that I had come across before, but it is a very nice one. This was a probing amendment to see what the Government's thinking was. I beg leave to withdraw the amendment.

Lord Goldsmith: On a previous occasion, I explained that the Bill preserves the current law on ratification of acts by directors but makes one significant change, which is that any decision to ratify conduct by a director that would amount to negligence, breach of duty, default or breach of trust in relation to the company must be taken by the members, without reliance of the votes of those members with a personal interest in the ratification. That is the effect of Clause 216 as amended on our previous day in Committee and it will be the law in relation to ratification in play under this provision. Where I take issue with him is on whether it is necessary to put in a further requirement that the ratification has to be effected in good faith in the interests of the company. With the exception of the change that we are making, our position remains that shareholders are able to exercise their votes as they please. That may include their own selfish, short-term reasons as well as what they believe to be in the best long-term interests of the company. Subject to the important change that is being made in Clause 216, we leave the rules on ratification for the purposes of derivative claims as they are in other parts of the Bill and do not wish to add the additional hurdle proposed by the noble Lord in this amendment.

Lord Goldsmith: I recognise this as a probing amendment; I am grateful, as it gives me the opportunity to explain a little more about the Government's thinking on Clause 242(3). Let me be quite direct in answering the noble Lord's question on how the court is to treat the factors. We expect the court, in exercising its discretion, to take into account all the factors set out; they are a mixture of the objective and the factual and we expect the court to consider them together. It would not be a question of taking it step by step in a particular order.

The test of whether a claim is a sensible one to bring—which is what paragraph (b) deals with—is objective, since, by definition, what is at issue is whether the director has acted properly. It is coupled with a series of tests which are designed to look at what the company actually wants rather than at what an abstract company would want. It has a sense of reality about it. How important each factor is in any particular case would, we envisage, be for the court to determine on the facts of the case, having regard to all the circumstances and all the factors that are set out. I hope that that goes some way towards answering the noble Lord's question.

Lord Hodgson of Astley Abbotts: I am grateful for that reply. Obviously, one of the issues, as the Attorney-General said, was the mixture of subjective and objective facts in the list. Lord Sharman moved Amendment No. 251A:

    Page 109, line 37, leave out ", and in the circumstances would be likely to be"

The noble Lord said: The purpose of tabling the amendment is to understand whether the Government wish to expand the purpose of granting permission to continue a derivative action beyond that which I understand to be embodied in the current law. The new statement of directors' duties in Clause 156(3) introduces an objective list of matters which directors must take into account in deciding whether a particular course of action is likely to promote the success of the company for the benefit of the members. It follows that derivative claims are more likely to be brought under the statutory procedure than under the current law. Given the impact of such claims and the management time likely to be absorbed, it is my view that Clause 242(3)(c) should not go further than the common law. I beg to move.

Lord Goldsmith: I think that I have already made it clear that the approach adopted in Part 11 deliberately introduces some changes to the law. The noble Lord is right that the ratifiability of an act or omission would no longer be an absolute bar to the continuation of an action. The wrongdoer control test would equally no longer apply.

We want the courts to look at what is really happening in the company. The court would take into account what will be the absolute bars to a case going forward and consider whether conduct has in fact been ratified or, as stated under subsection (2)(a), whether a person acting in accordance with the duty to promote the success of the company,

    "would not seek to continue the claim".

It could also include the provision in paragraph (b) where an act has not yet occurred but the act or omission has been authorised by the company. Once the claim gets over those initial hurdles, the court would adopt this second stage and the discretion of the court would come into play, having regard to all the factors listed in subsection (3). This would include paragraph (c), which states,

    "where the cause of action results from an act or omission that is yet to occur, whether the act or omission could be, and . . . would be likely to be",

authorised or ratified, and paragraph (d), which states,

    "where the cause of action arises from an act or omission that has already occurred, whether the act or omission could be, and in the circumstances would be likely to be, ratified by the company".

So the court would take into account the possibility that the act or omission could be ratified. When commenting on something that my noble friend Lord Grabiner said, I mentioned that the court might adjourn the proceedings for something to happen. One possibility is that the court might decide in certain circumstances to adjourn to see whether the act was ratified. I am not saying that it would have to, but that is a possibility.
 
27 Feb 2006 : Column GC28
 

I would resist an absolute bar—which is what the noble Lord's amendment would achieve—because an action can be ratified. Rather, I would put it in terms that if it has been ratified it is a bar, but otherwise it is a matter for the court to take into account and, in particular, to take into account the likelihood that it would be ratified.
